        <title>Unravelling the genome of Holy basil: an “incomparable” “elixir of life” of traditional Indian medicine | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="
                  Ocimum sanctum L. (O. tenuiflorum) family-Lamiaceae is an important component of Indian tradition of medicine as well as culture around the world, and hence is known as &#8220;Holy basil&#8221; in India. This plant is mentioned in the ancient texts of Ayurveda as an &#8220;elixir of life&#8221; (life saving) herb and worshipped for over 3000 years due to its healing properties.  Although used in various ailments, validation of molecules for differential activities is yet to be fully analyzed, as about 80 % of the patents on this plant are on extracts or the plant parts, and mainly focussed on essential oil components. With a view to understand the full metabolic potential of this plant whole nuclear and chloroplast genomes were sequenced for the first time combining the sequence data from 4 libraries and three NGS platforms. The saturated draft assembly of the genome was about 386 Mb, along with the plastid genome of 142,245 bp, turning out to be the smallest in Lamiaceae. In addition to SSR markers, 136 proteins were identified as homologous to five important plant genomes. Pathway analysis indicated an abundance of phenylpropanoids in O. sanctum. Phylogenetic analysis for chloroplast proteome placed Salvia miltiorrhiza as the nearest neighbor. Comparison of the chemical compounds and genes availability in O. sanctum and S. miltiorrhiza indicated the potential for the discovery of new active molecules. The genome sequence and annotation of O. sanctum provides new insights into the function of genes and the medicinal nature of the metabolites synthesized in this plant. This information is highly beneficial for mining biosynthetic pathways for important metabolites in related species."/>

        <!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Unravelling the genome of Holy basil: an “incomparable” “elixir of life” of traditional Indian medicine"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Ocimum sanctum L. (O. tenuiflorum) family-Lamiaceae is an important component of Indian tradition of medicine as well as culture around the world, and hence is known as “Holy basil” in India. This plant is mentioned in the ancient texts of Ayurveda as an “elixir of life” (life saving) herb and worshipped for over 3000 years due to its healing properties. Although used in various ailments, validation of molecules for differential activities is yet to be fully analyzed, as about 80 % of the patents on this plant are on extracts or the plant parts, and mainly focussed on essential oil components. With a view to understand the full metabolic potential of this plant whole nuclear and chloroplast genomes were sequenced for the first time combining the sequence data from 4 libraries and three NGS platforms. The saturated draft assembly of the genome was about 386 Mb, along with the plastid genome of 142,245 bp, turning out to be the smallest in Lamiaceae. In addition to SSR markers, 136 proteins were identified as homologous to five important plant genomes. Pathway analysis indicated an abundance of phenylpropanoids in O. sanctum. Phylogenetic analysis for chloroplast proteome placed Salvia miltiorrhiza as the nearest neighbor. Comparison of the chemical compounds and genes availability in O. sanctum and S. miltiorrhiza indicated the potential for the discovery of new active molecules. The genome sequence and annotation of O. sanctum provides new insights into the function of genes and the medicinal nature of the metabolites synthesized in this plant. This information is highly beneficial for mining biosynthetic pathways for important metabolites in related species."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Unravelling the genome of Holy basil: an “incomparable” “elixir of life” of traditional Indian medicine</h1>
                        <ul class="c-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list"><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Shubhra-Rastogi" data-author-popup="auth-Shubhra-Rastogi">Shubhra Rastogi</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="CSIR-Central Institute of Medicinal and Aromatic Plants, P.O.                     <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>
                  <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> L. (<i>O. tenuiflorum</i>) family-Lamiaceae is an important component of Indian tradition of medicine as well as culture around the world, and hence is known as “Holy basil” in India. This plant is mentioned in the ancient texts of Ayurveda as an “elixir of life” (life saving) herb and worshipped for over 3000 years due to its healing properties.  Although used in various ailments, validation of molecules for differential activities is yet to be fully analyzed, as about 80 % of the patents on this plant are on extracts or the plant parts, and mainly focussed on essential oil components. With a view to understand the full metabolic potential of this plant whole nuclear and chloroplast genomes were sequenced for the first time combining the sequence data from 4 libraries and three NGS platforms.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>The saturated draft assembly of the genome was about 386 Mb, along with the plastid genome of 142,245 bp, turning out to be the smallest in Lamiaceae. In addition to SSR markers, 136 proteins were identified as homologous to five important plant genomes. Pathway analysis indicated an abundance of phenylpropanoids in <i>O. sanctum</i>. Phylogenetic analysis for chloroplast proteome placed <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> as the nearest neighbor. Comparison of the chemical compounds and genes availability in <i>O. sanctum</i> and <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> indicated the potential for the discovery of new active molecules.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusion</h3><p>The genome sequence and annotation of <i>O. sanctum</i> provides new insights into the function of genes and the medicinal nature of the metabolites synthesized in this plant. This information is highly beneficial for mining biosynthetic pathways for important metabolites in related species.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>
                <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> L. (<i>O. tenuiflorum</i>) is an important sacred medicinal plant of India known as “holy basil”, <i>Thulasi</i>, <i>Vishnupriya,</i> and <i>Tulsi</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Darrah HH. The cultivated basils. Independence, MO: Buckeye Printing Company; 1980." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16613e881">1</a>] and worshipped for over more than 3000 years [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gupta SK, Prakash J, Srivastava S. Validation of traditional claim of Tulsi. Ocimum sanctum Linn. as a medicinal plant. Indian J Exp Biol. 2002;40(7):765–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d16613e884">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference  3" title="Uma Devi P. Radioprotective, anticarcinogenic and antioxidant properties of the Indian holy basil, Ocimum sanctum (Tulasi). Indian J Exp Biol. 2001;39(3):185–90." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d16613e887"> 3</a>]. This herb is popular in traditional medicine as “The Queen of Herbs,” “The Incomparable One,” and “The Mother Medicine of Nature” [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Singh N, Hoette Y. Tulsi: The Mother Medicine of Nature. Lucknow, India: International Institute of Herbal Medicine; 2002." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16613e890">4</a>]. Being legendary sacred basil (Tulsi), is recognized [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Warrier PK: In: Indian Medicinal Plants. Edited by Longman O. New Delhi: CBS publication; 1995:168" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d16613e893">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference  6" title="Pattanayak P, Behera P, Das D, Panda SK. Ocimum sanctum Linn. A reservoir plant for therapeutic applications: An overview. Pharmacogn Rev. 2010;4(7):95–105." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16613e896"> 6</a>] not only for its sanctity, but forms an indispensible part of the traditional herbal medicine of East as discussed in Ayurvedic text of Charaka Samhita as well as Unani medicinal system. It is native to India and parts of northern and eastern Africa, Hainan Island, and Taiwan, and grows wild throughout India up to an altitude of 5900 ft (1800 m) in the Himalayas [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="World Health Organization. Folium Ocimi Sancti. In: WHO Monographs on Selected Medicinal Plants, vol. 2. Geneva, Switzerland: World Health Organization; 2002. p. 206–16." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d16613e900">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gupta AK, Tandon N, Sharma M. Ocimum sanctum Linn. In: Gupta AK, Tandon N, Sharma M, editors. Quality Standards of Indian Medicinal Plants, Volume 5. New Delhi, India: Medicinal Plants Unit, Indian Council of Medical Research; 2008. p. 275–84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16613e903">9</a>]. The leaf of the plant owes a stronger, somewhat pungent taste than other basils due to a sesquiterpenoid <i>beta-</i>caryophyllene, and a phenylpropanoid eugenol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bhasin M. Ocimum- Taxonomy, medicinal potentialities and economic value of essential oil. Journal of Biosphere. 2012;1:48–50." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16613e909">10</a>]. <i>O. sanctum</i> has been suggested to possess anti-fertility, anti-cancer, anti-diabetic, anti-fungal, anti-microbial, cardioprotective, analgesic, anti-spasmodic and adaptogenic actions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Pattanayak P, Behera P, Das D, Panda SK. Ocimum sanctum Linn. A reservoir plant for therapeutic applications: An overview. Pharmacogn Rev. 2010;4(7):95–105." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16613e915">6</a>]. The chemical composition of Tulsi is highly complex, containing many biologically active phytochemicals with variable proportions among varieties or even plants within the same field. The volatile oil of leaf [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kelm MA, Nair MG, Strasburg GM, DeWitt DL. Antioxidant and cyclooxygenase inhibitory phenolic compounds from Ocimum sanctum Linn. Phytomedicine. 2000;7:7–13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d16613e919">11</a>] contains eugenol (1-hydroxy-2-methoxy-4-allylbenzene), euginal, urosolic acid [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Shishodia S, Majumdar S, Banerjee S, Aggarwal BB. Urosolic acid inhibits nuclear factor-kappaB activation induced by carcinogenic agents through suppression of IkappaBalpha kinase and p65 phosphorylation: Correlation with down-regulation of cyclooxygenase 2, matrix metalloproteinase 9, and cyclin D1. Cancer Res. 2003;63:4375–83." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16613e922">12</a>], carvacrol, limatrol, caryophyllene, methyl carvicol while the seed volatile oil has fatty acids and sitosterol. In addition, the seed mucilage contains some levels of sugars and the anthocyans are present in green leaves [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Pattanayak P, Behera P, Das D, Panda SK. Ocimum sanctum Linn. A reservoir plant for therapeutic applications: An overview. Pharmacogn Rev. 2010;4(7):95–105." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16613e925">6</a>]. The leaf volatiles (terpenes and phenylpropenes) are synthesized and sequestered in glandular hairs present on the leaves, also known as peltate trichomes, which are the characteristic of lamiaceae members [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Iijima Y, Gang DR, Fridman E, Lewinsohn E, Pichersky E. Characterization of geraniol synthase from the peltate glands of sweet basil. Plant Physiol. 2004;134:370–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16613e928">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tissier A. Trichome Specific Expression: Promoters and Their Applications. Transgenic Plants -Advances and Limitations. 2012;353–378." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d16613e931">14</a>]. Two types of <i>O. sanctum</i> L. are used for cultivation: (i) plants with green leaves known as Sri/ Rama Tulsi &amp; (ii) plants with purple leaves known as Krishna/ Shyama Tulsi [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Anonymous. Wealth of India. In: Publication and Information Directorate. New Delhi, India: CSIR; 1991. p. 79–89." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d16613e938">8</a>]. Furthermore, the quantity of many of its constituents can be significantly altered by varying conditions used for growing; harvesting, processing and storage that are not yet well understood [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rahman S, Islam R, Kamruzzaman, Alam K, Jamal AHM. Ocimum sanctum L.: A review of phytochemical and pharmacological profile. American Journal of Drug Discovery and Development 2011, ISSN 2150-427x / doi:10.3923/ajdd.2011." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16613e941">15</a>]. All of the varieties of <i>Ocimum</i> have unique and individual chemical compositions; but their medicinal properties are not yet explored completely. Despite huge importance of <i>Ocimum</i>, very little transcriptomic and genomic data of <i>Ocimum</i> sp. is available limiting studies on important phytochemical pathways. But comparative transcriptome analysis of <i>Ocimum</i> species (<i>O. sanctum</i> and <i>O. basilicum</i>) was recently reported [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e963">16</a>]. This report correlated higher digital expression of phenylpropanoid/ terpenoid pathway genes of <i>O. basilicum</i> to higher essential oil content and chromosome number (<i>O. sanctum</i>, 2n = 16; and <i>O. basilicum</i>, 2n = 48). Also several cytochrome P450s (26) and transcription factor families (40) were identified which could be utilized to characterize genes related to secondary metabolism and its regulation.</p><p>Hence, there was a need to know about the genome of this plant to understand its metabolic potential, diversity, regulation and evolutionary implications. Here, we report the draft nuclear genome sequence of 386 Mb and the plastid of 142,245 bp sequenced with a composite next generation sequencing technologies. On the basis of assembly, 53,480 protein coding genes were identified. Gene model prediction revealed the similarity of <i>O. sanctum</i> genome to <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> and <i>Solanum lycopersicum,</i> all sharing same sub-class (asterid)<i>.</i>
              </p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly and validation</h3><p>A whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy by generating long and short paired-end reads, along with long reads and mate-pair libraries was applied to assemble the 386 Mb genome sequence of <i>O. sanctum.</i> The process workflow of the same has been provided in the Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig1">1</a>. Two libraries (long and short reads) of Illumina HiSeq2000, one library of 454 GS FLX and one mate-pair library of SOLiD 5500XL were constructed. Illumina libraries were used to generate the contigs and Illumina paired-end data along with 454 GS FLX single end data were used for contig merging as a result of which scaffolds were generated. While Illumina generated 45.37 Gb data (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM1">1</a>), 454 sequencing resulted in 320.3 Mb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM2">2</a>) data and SOLiD generated 12.68 Gb (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM3">3</a>) data representing approximately ~130-fold coverage of the predicted <i>O. sanctum</i> genome. With the help of the two Illumina library data (short insert and long insert) the assembly showed significant improvement in respect of N50. Long- and short-paired end reads from Illumina deep sequencing was used to assemble a total of 107,785 contigs into 22,776 scaffolds. Super-scaffolding was performed in order to merge the existing gap-closed scaffolds into super-scaffolds using relative orientation of SOLiD mate pair reads. Finally, 9059 super-scaffolds of maximum length upto 2,211,552 bp were generated (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Tab1">1</a>). Out of the total super-scaffolds generated, 4159 super-scaffolds are larger than or equal to 1 kb in length (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Tab1">1</a>). The N50 length of contigs, scaffolds and super-scaffolds was found to be 12,769 bp, 61,854 bp and 303,233 bp respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Tab1">1</a>). The total length of the gaps in the assembled scaffolds was 26.11 Mb in a total of 3999 super-scaffolds. The total number of gaps present is 45,803 considering even the presence of a single N as one gap. The biggest gap identified was of 4906 bp in length. Mate-pair reads significantly closed ~60 % of gaps between scaffolds and five-fold change in N50 value and N90 value was also observed where N50 increased from 61,242 to 303,233 while N90 from 12,534 to 73,672. In order to validate the genome assembly, a total of 69,117 transcripts generated from our previous study [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1030">16</a>] were mapped to the genome data and more than 95 % transcripts (66,891) showed 100 % coverage (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM4">4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Process workflow of <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> whole genome sequencing and assembly</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics of contigs and scaffolds generated using the three sequencing platforms Illumina HiSeq2000, 454 GS FLX and SOLiD 5500XL</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">
                  <i>De-novo</i> assembly of chloroplast and mitochondria genome data</h3><p>The complete chloroplast (cp) genome of <i>O. sanctum</i> is 142,524 bp in length (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig2">2</a>). Recently, Qian et al [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Qian J, Song J, Gao H, Zhu Y, Xu J, Pang X, et al. The Complete Chloroplast Genome Sequence of the Medicinal Plant Salvia miltiorrhiza. PLoS One. 2013;8(2):e57607. doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0057607." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1447">17</a>] had reported the chloroplast genome of <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> to be the smallest with the exception of <i>Epifagus virginiana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Wolfe KH, Morden CW, Palmer JD. Function and evolution of a minimal plastid genome from a nonphotosynthetic parasitic plant. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 1992;89(22):10648–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1457">18</a>] cp genome of order lamiales. But this investigation revealed <i>O. sanctum</i> cp genome to be 8804 bp smaller than <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> (member of the <i>Ocimum</i> family- lamiaceae) cp genome of length 151,328 bp. Hence <i>O. sanctum</i> cp genome is now reported as the smallest of the Lamiales cp genomes as it is ~8800 bp smaller than <i>E. virginiana</i> cp genome. The overall GC content of the <i>O. sanctum</i> cp genome is 36.2 %, which is similar to the other reported asterid cp genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Qian J, Song J, Gao H, Zhu Y, Xu J, Pang X, et al. The Complete Chloroplast Genome Sequence of the Medicinal Plant Salvia miltiorrhiza. PLoS One. 2013;8(2):e57607. doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0057607." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1479">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference  19" title="Yi DK, Kim KJ. Complete chloroplast genome sequences of important oilseed crop Sesamum indicum L. PLoS One. 2012;7, e35872." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1482"> 19</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kim KJ, Lee HL. Complete chloroplast genome sequences from Korean ginseng (Panax schinseng Nees) and comparative analysis of sequence evolution among 17 vascular plants. DNA Res. 2004;11:247–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1485">22</a>]. The <i>O. sanctum</i> cp genome was found to code a total 158 genes, including 43 transfer RNA (tRNA) genes and four ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes. The aligned reads of cp genome of <i>O. sanctum</i> to other angiosperms (referred in materials and methods section) were assembled into contigs for finally generating the scaffolds using all the Illumina data. Similar procedure was carried out for mitochondrial genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM5">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM6">6</a>) assembly except considering <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> as the reference mitochondial genome. A total of 48 scaffolds from 140 contigs from cp genome, and 41 scaffolds from 124 contigs (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM5">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM6">6</a>) from the mitochondrial genome got generated.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Gene map of the <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> chloroplast genome Genes drawn inside the circle are transcribed clockwise, and those outside are counterclockwise. Genes belonging to different functional groups are color-coded. The darker gray in the inner circle corresponds to GC content, while the lighter gray corresponds to AT content</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genomic composition and SSR prediction</h3><p>GC content is an important indicator of the genomic composition including evolution, gene structure (intron size and number), gene regulation and stability of DNA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Carels N, Hatey P, Jabbari K, Bernardi G. Compositional properties of homologous coding sequences from plants. J Mol Evol. 1998;46:45–53." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1540">23</a>]. Average GC content of <i>O. sanctum</i> was 38.37 %. Earlier researchers have reported that across the broad phylogenetic sweep, genome size may be correlated with intron size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wendel JF, Cronn RC, Alvarez I, Liu B, Small RL, Senchina DS. Intron Size and Genome Size in Plants. Mol Biol Evol. 2002;19(12):2346–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1546">24</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Vinogradov AE. Intron-genome size relationship on a large evolutionary scale. J Mol Evol. 1999;49:376–84." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1549">26</a>], suggesting that some fraction of genome size evolution takes place within genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Mclysaght A, Enright AJ, Skrabanek L, Wolfe KH. Estimation of synteny conservation and genome compaction between pufferfish (Fugu) and human. Yeast. 2000;17:22–36." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1552">27</a>]. While performing the annotation of gene models, taking <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> as references, it was found that the percent genes containing introns from these plants were 55.5 % and 64.5 %, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM7">7</a>). It has been observed that introns and their positions are highly conserved during land plant evolution excluding conifers [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zimmer AD, Lang D, Buchta K, Rombauts S, Nishiyama T, Hasebe M, et al. Reannotation and extended community resources for the genome of the non-seed plant Physcomitrella patens provide insights into the evolution of plant gene structures and functions. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:498." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1565">28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Teich R, Grauvogel C, Petersen J. Intron distribution in Plantae: 500 million years of stasis during land plant evolution. Gene. 2007;394:96–104." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1568">29</a>].</p><p>Comparative studies had revealed that intron lengths and the abundance of mobile repetitive elements have a direct correlation with genome size, such that large genomes have longer introns and a higher proportion of mobile elements [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lynch M, Conery JS. The origins of genome complexity. Science. 2003;302:1401–4." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1574">30</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sena JS, Giguère I, Boyle B, Rigault P, Birol I, Zuccolo A, et al. Evolution of gene structure in the conifer Picea glauca: a comparative analysis of the impact of intron size. BMC Plant Biol. 2014;14:95." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1577">31</a>]. Intron sizes in the genes of <i>O. sanctum</i> ranged from 5 bp to 8000 bp (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM7">7</a>). A reason for intron size variation among organisms may be due to inherent mutational processes generating insertions and deletions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wendel JF, Cronn RC, Alvarez I, Liu B, Small RL, Senchina DS. Intron Size and Genome Size in Plants. Mol Biol Evol. 2002;19(12):2346–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1586">24</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Ogata H, Fujibuchi W, Kanehisa M. The size differences among mammalian introns are due to the accumulation of small deletions. FEBS Lett. 1996;390:99–103." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1590">32</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Petrov DA, Sangster TA, Johnston JS, Hartl DL, Shaw KL. Evidence for DNA loss as a determinant of genome size. Science. 2000;287:1060–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1593">35</a>]. It was also reported that low distribution of recombination regions leads to increased intron size [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Lynch M. Intron evolution as a population-genetic process. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2002;99:6118–23." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1596">36</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Comeron JM, Kreitman M. The correlation between intron length and recombination in Drosophila: dynamic equilibrium between mutational and selective forces. Genetics. 2000;156:1175–90." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1599">37</a>].</p><p>Among different classes of molecular markers, microsatellite or simple sequence repeat (SSR) markers are the most preferred for its array of applications in plant genetics and breeding due to their multi-allelic nature, reproducibility, co-dominant inheritance with high abundance and wide genome coverage [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gupta S, Shukla R, Roy S, Sen N, Sharma A. In silico SSR and FDM analysis through EST sequences in Ocimum basilicum. Plant Omics Journal. 2010;3(4):121–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1605">38</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Gupta PK, Varshney RK. The development and use of microsatellite markers for genetic analysis and plant breeding with emphasis on bread wheat. Euphytica. 2000;113:163–85." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1608">39</a>]. A total of 4827 sequences greater than 500 bp length were examined for SSR search out of which 2612 sequences were found having SSR repeats while 2364 sequences showed the presence of more than one SSR (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig3">3</a>). A sum of 142,601 SSRs were predicted, with the highest being mono-repeats (85,624) and (13,389) complex SSR’s. The sequences were checked for mono-repeats occurring at-least 10 times, di-repeats occurring at-least 6 times and tri/ tetra/ penta/ hexa-repeats occurring atleast 5 times. The SSR was classified as complex when two SSR’s were present within 100 bp distance of each other. On the other side, 1,166,753 sequences of less than 500 bp were identified, only 162 SSRs highest being mono-repeats (68) followed by complex SSR’s (57) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig3">3</a>). Previously, we reported SSRs from the transcriptome of <i>O. sanctum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1621">16</a>] but the SSRs identified were very few in comparison to the present report. Similarly, efforts were made by researchers towards the development of molecular markers in order to carry out genetic diversity studies on <i>Ocimum sp.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Carovic-Stanko K, Liber Z, Besendorfer V, Javornik B, Bohanec B, Kolak I, et al. Genetic relations among basil taxa (Ocimum L.) based on molecular markers, nuclear DNA content, and chromosome number. Plant Syst Evol. 2010;285:13–22." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1627">40</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Mahajan V, Rather IA, Awasthi P, Anand R, Gairola S, Meena SR, et al. Development of chemical and EST-SSR markers for Ocimum genus. Industrial Crops and Products 2014, In Press, doi:10.1016/j.indcrop.2014.10.052." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1630">42</a>]. But, SSRs reported from the present study with a large data set would be helpful in providing insights to the plant breeders and geneticists for evaluation of desired genotypes with varied essential oil compositions and also for further development of new species of <i>Ocimum</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Frequency distribution of SSRs based on motif types. p1: Mono-nucleotide repeats; p2: Di-nucleotide repeats; p3: Tri-nucleotide repeats, p4: Tetra-nucleotide repeats; p5: Penta-nucleotide repeats; p6: Hexa-nucleotide repeats; Complex: no. of SSRs involved in compound formation</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>A gene density of ~30 genes per 100 kb and ~20 genes per 100 kb was observed in <i>O. sanctum</i> gene model prediction taking <i>N. tabacum</i> (tobacco) and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (tomato), respectively as references. Since <i>O. sanctum</i> is a small genome plant, the gene density is similar to that of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> i.e., upto 38 genes per 100 kb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Initiative TAG. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature. 2000;408:796–815." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1673">43</a>]. Large genomes like barley and wheat show a gene density of about 5 genes per 23 kb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Feuillet C, Keller B. High gene density is conserved at syntenic loci of small and large grass genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 1999;96:8265–70." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1676">44</a>] as it was suggested that the larger genomes would have accumulated non-coding sequences between the single-copy genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Bennetzen JL, SanMiguel P, Chen M, Tikhonov A, Francki M, Avramova Z. Grass genomes. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 1999;95:1975–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1679">45</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>In order to assign putative functions to the predicted genes of <i>O. sanctum</i>, they were compared against the NR (non-redundant) protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis</i>. The associated hits were searched for their respective GO. Based on sequence homology, 85,723 protein sequences were categorized into 31 functional groups under three main categories: biological processes (BP), cellular components (CC) and molecular functions (MF) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig4">4</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM8">8</a>). Genes were predicted from 22,776 scaffolds by mapping (BLASTP) the predicted proteins with UNIPROT with all Viridiplantae clade protein sequences. Out of 85,723 protein coding loci from 22,776 scaffolds, a total of 53,480 were annotated with UNIPROT (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM9">9</a>) but only 22,270 protein coding genes were found to be unique. On observing the plant species distribution of hits to UNIPROT database, maximum hits were from the plant <i>Genlisea aurea</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM10">10</a>) which is one of the smallest known genome among higher plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Leushkin EV, Sutormin RA, Nabieva ER, Penin AA, Kondrashov AS, Logacheva MD. The miniature genome of a carnivorous plant Genlisea aurea contains a low number of genes and short non-coding sequences. BMC Genomics. 2013;14:476." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1712">46</a>]. The number of unique protein coding genes (22,270) in the <i>O. sanctum</i> genome, was in range as reported in potato, tomato [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium: The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 2012, 485: doi:10.1038/nature11119." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1718">47</a>], neem [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Krishnan NM, Pattnaik S, Jain P, Gaur P, Choudhary R, Vaidyanathan S, et al. A draft of the genome and four transcriptomes of a medicinal and pesticidal angiosperm Azadirachta indica. BMC Genomics. 2012;13:464." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1721">48</a>] and grapevine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="The French-Italian Public Consortium for Grapevine Genome characterization. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 2007, 449: doi:10.1038/nature06148." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1725">49</a>], having 35,004, 34,727, 20,000 and 30,434 protein coding genes, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Pie-chart showing top 10 functional classes in each of the 3 categories of gene ontology classification. The three main categories are: biological process, cellular component and molecular function representing the assignment <i>O. sanctum</i> predicted proteins with BLAST matches in NR (non-redundant) protein sequences of <i>Arabidopsis</i> to each GO term</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>
                  <i>Ab initio</i> gene model prediction was performed on scaffold sequences utilizing minimal information from the nearest available species. Overall, 130,526 and 87,918 proteins were predicted using training sets of <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> respectively. A total of 65,935 proteins were common between the two predictions. Gene annotation of the predicted proteins with BLASTP resulted in annotation of 80,516 NR proteins. A set of 38,868 of these annotated proteins were common to the predictions from <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>S. Lycopersicum</i>, respectively. The un-annotated predicted proteins were scanned with Pfam and another 18,940 proteins got annotated with a predicted domain signature. Database annotation of assembled scaffold sequences greater than 500 bp was carried out for matching with the EST/mRNA sequences available for <i>Ocimum</i> in the NCBI databases (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM11">11</a>). A total of 23,420 EST and 52 mRNA were queried, with a match to the assembled scaffolds for 21,984 of the EST/mRNA sequences at greater than 90 % sequence identity. Also <i>Arabidopsis</i> sequences (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM12">12</a>) from TAIR database and <i>N. tabacum</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM13">13</a>) and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM14">14</a>) sequences from NCBI were Blast- checked against the <i>O. sanctum</i> scaffolds with percent hitting scaffolds of 34.65 %, 4.9 % and 5.29 %, respectively. Database annotation of EST/mRNA from NCBI datasets identified the mitochondria and chloroplast expressed proteins. All of the 392 scaffolds identified were annotated to potentially map to these sequences (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM15">15</a>). Out of 392 scaffolds, 270 were redundant and only 122 were non-redundant. On the basis of annotation of chloroplast and mitochondria encoded proteins against TAIR database, it was found that out of 122 non-redundant scaffolds of <i>O. sanctum</i>, 95 were chloroplastic while remaining 27 were mitochondrial.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p> To identify the phylogenetic position of <i>O. sanctum</i> within the asterid lineage, multiple sequence alignments was performed using 63 protein-coding genes (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM16">16</a>) commonly present in the 32 complete cp genomes representing 10 families within five orders of asterids including Apiaceae, Araliaceae, Asteraceae, Convolvulaceae, Gesneriaceae, Lamiaceae, Oleaceae, Pedaliaceae, Rubiaceae and Solanaceae (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM17">17</a>). Two additional eudicot cp genomes, <i>Spinacia olerace</i>a and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, were set as outgroups. A phylogenetic tree was generated using maximum parsimony and maximum likelihood method (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig5">5</a>). Bootstrap analysis showed that there were 25 out of 31 nodes with bootstrap values &gt;95 %, and 14 of these had a bootstrap value of 100 %. The tree topologies formed two major clades, euasterids I and II. The results strongly supported the position of <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> and <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> with 100 % bootstrap from the same family lamiaceae as the sister of the closely related species <i>Sesamum indicum</i> and <i>Boea hygrometrica</i> in the order Lamiales. As the chloroplast genome is considered to be free from evolutionary processes, like gene duplication/ deletion, intensive evolution and pseudogene formation, which are characteristically frequent among nuclear genes, this was included in the phylogenetic analysis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Clegg MT, Zurawski G. Chloroplast DNA and the Study of Plant Phylogeny: Present Status and Future Prospects. In: Soltis PS, Soltis DE, Dpyle JJ, Editors. Molecular Systematics of Plants. New York: Springer US; 1992. 1-13." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1846">50</a>]. Slow rate of sequence evolution in chloroplast DNA is appropriate to include cp genome in phylogenetic studies of the highly cross pollinated plant like <i>O. sanctum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Palmer JD. Chloroplast DNA and Molecular Phylogeny. Bioessays. 1985;2(6):263–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1853">51</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree of the asterid clade based on 63 protein-coding genes of chloroplast genome. Numbers above each node are bootstrap support values. <i>Spinacea oleracea</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were set as outgroups</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Pathway identification</h3><p>To identify the biological pathways functional in <i>O. sanctum</i> 85,723 protein sequences from scaffolds were mapped to the reference canonical pathways in KEGG taking <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i> as reference organisms, out of which only 6328 proteins got predicted in KAAS (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM18">18</a>). All transcripts were classified mainly under five categories: metabolism, cellular processes, genetic information processing, environmental information processing and others. Highest numbers of sequences were related to metabolism. Maximum percentage of the sequences fell under the category of phenylpropanoid biosynthesis. <i>O. sanctum</i> is good source of phenylpropene- eugenol and is one of the compounds which attributes to its medicinal property [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Shukla A, Kaur K, Ahuja P. Tulsi the Medicinal Value. Online International Interdisciplinary Research Journal. 2013;3(2):9–14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1905">52</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Prakash P, Gupta N. Therapeutic uses of Ocimum sanctum Linn (Tulsi) with a note on eugenol and its pharmacological actions: a short review. Indian J Physiol Pharmacol. 2005;49(2):125–31." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1908">53</a>]. Precursor molecules for phenylpropanoid biosynthesis are derived from the shikimate pathway while terpenoid biosynthesis utilizes isoprenoid precursors from cytosolic MVA (mevalonate) as well as plastidial MEP pathways (2-Cmethyl-D-erythritol 4-phosphate/1-deoxy-D-xylulose 5-phosphate/non-mevalonate pathways) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1911">16</a>]. On sorting 53,480 protein coding genes of <i>O. sanctum</i> annotated from UNIPROT for phenylpropanoid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig6">6</a>) and terpenoid (MEP and MVA) pathway genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig6">6</a>) it was found that the highest number of phenylpropanoid pathway genes were identified as compared to the terpenes. Since the <i>O. sanctum</i> variety used in the present study is high-yielding, eugenol-rich, oil producing variety [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Lal RK, Khanuja SPS, Agnihotri AK, Misra HO, Shasany AK, Naqvi AA, et al. High yielding eugenol rich oil producing variety of Ocimum sanctum – CIM-Ayu. J Med Arom Plant Sci. 2003;25:746–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1927">54</a>] with 83 % eugenol in the oil, it correlates with the presence of higher number of phenylpropanoid pathway genes. Interestingly, highest number transcripts of copalyl diphosphate synthase (CPS) were present among the mevalonate pathway genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#Fig6">6</a>). This enzyme participates in gibberellin biosynthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Fall RR, West CA. Purification and properties of kaurene synthetase from Fusarium moniliforme. J Biol Chem. 1971;246(22):6913–28." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1933">55</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig6_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_Fig6_HTML.gif" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Abundance of phenylpropanoid, mevalonate and non- mevalonate pathway genes as per the annotation of predicted genes against all Viridiplantae clade genes in Uniprot.
