                <p>Not only gibberellins, but a wide range of secondary metabolites, including terpenes and alkaloids, are also derived either from <i>ent</i>-copalyl pyrophosphate itself or from <i>ent</i>-kaurene or <i>ent</i>-kaurenoic acid, the next two intermediates in the metabolic pathway to gibberellins. Knowledge of these secondary metabolic pathways is very much limited as compared to gibberellin biosynthetic pathway, and is often little more than a speculation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="BRENDA [&#xA;                    http://www.brenda-enzymes.org/enzyme.php?ecno=4.2.3.19&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d16613e1998">56</a>]. Further functional characterization studies for copalyl diphosphate synthase may help in proving the possibility of CPS involvement in terpene and alkaloid biosynthesis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Medicinal nature of <i>O. sanctum</i>
                </h3><p>In this analysis <i>O. sanctum</i> cp genome was observed to be evolutionarily nearest to <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i>. In the absence of complete genome sequence data (unfinished draft genome) of <i>S. Miltiorrhiza</i>, the chloroplast genome comparison analysis was taken into account. Both the plants are used widely in two different traditional medicine systems (Indian and Chinese, respectively), and hence may be implicated for similar molecules, activities <i>vis a vis</i> the genes biosynthesizing metabolites. In addition, both plants have chromosome number described to be 2n = 16 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2025">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Zhao HX, Zhang L, Fan X, Yang RW, Ding CB, Zhou YH. Studies on chromosome numbers of Salvia miltiorrhiza, S. flava and S. evansiana. Zhongguo Zhong Yao Za Zhi. 2006;31:1847–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2029">57</a>]. The active ingredients in <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> are both hydrophilic (phenolic acids like rosmarinic acid, salvianolic acid B, lithospermic acid and dihydroxyphenyllactic acid) and lipophilic diterpene components (tanshinones, including structurally related tanshinone I, tanshinone IIA, cryptotanshinone, and dihydrotanshinone I) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Yang L, Ding G, Lin H, Cheng H, Kong Y, Wei Y, et al. Transcriptome analysis of medicinal plant Salvia miltiorrhiza and identification of genes related to tanshinone biosynthesis. PLoS One. 2013;8(11), e80464." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2035">58</a>]. These molecules are responsible for a wide array of activities like anti-bacterial, anti-oxidative and anti-viral to hepatoprotective activities. The chemical composition of Tulsi is highly complex, and the important are triterpene like urosolic acid (cardioprotective effect), phenolics like rosmarinic acid, apigenin, cirsimaritin, isothymusin and isothymonin (exhibiting antioxidant and anti-inflammatory activities), and important aroma components like 1, 8 cineole, linalool, methyl chavicol (estragole) and eugenol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Rastogi S, Meena S, Bhattacharya A, Ghosh S, Shukla RK, Sangwan NS, et al. De novo sequencing and comparative analysis of holy and sweet basil transcriptomes. BMC Genomics. 2014;15:588." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2038">16</a>]. Phenolic acid compounds production by hairy root culture have been reported in both <i>O. basilicum</i> and <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Hao G, Ji H, Li Y, Shi R, Wang J, Feng L, et al. Exogenous ABA and polyamines enhanced salvianolic acids contents in hairy root cultures of Salvia miltiorrhiza Bge. f.alba. Plant Omics. 2012;5:446–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2048">59</a>]. In addition, the vast literature indicates phenylpropanoid derivatives in these two plants are responsible for a range of major activities. In this investigation also we could observe the dominance of phenylpropanoid pathway genes. The highest number of sequences among the mevalonate pathway genes in <i>O. sanctum</i> are observed to be homologous to copalyl diphosphate synthases (CPS), that are involved in the biosynthesis of an important bioactive diterpene tanshinone in <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Yang L, Ding G, Lin H, Cheng H, Kong Y, Wei Y, et al. Transcriptome analysis of medicinal plant Salvia miltiorrhiza and identification of genes related to tanshinone biosynthesis. PLoS One. 2013;8(11), e80464." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2057">58</a>]. As <i>O. sanctum</i> is traditionally used for many aliments and the compounds of this plant are not fully investigated, the possibility exists for the discovery of tanshinone like compounds and other novel diterpenes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>The genome of Holy basil, assembled <i>de novo</i> in this study, presents the smallest nuclear genome in the family Lamiaceae and smallest cp genome in the order Lamiales. Phylogenetically, <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> is most similar to <i>O. sanctum</i> with a reported genome size of approximately ~600 Mb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Qian J, Song J, Gao H, Zhu Y, Xu J, Pang X, et al. The Complete Chloroplast Genome Sequence of the Medicinal Plant Salvia miltiorrhiza. PLoS One. 2013;8(2):e57607. doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0057607." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2081">17</a>]. Although, both <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> and <i>O. sanctum</i> predominantly produce phenylpropanoids and both have the identical diploid number of chromosome (2n = 16), the genome size of <i>O. sanctum</i> is little more than half of the genome size of <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i>. Hence, <i>O. sanctum</i> genome (386 Mb) seems to be quite compact with relatively less repeat sequences, even though it falls in the identical phylogenetic clade. In contrast to the genome sizes of the plants used in the gene model prediction like <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (~900 Mb) and <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> (~4567 Mb), <i>O. sanctum</i> genome (~386 Mb) falls in the category of the plants with small genome and is just 1.5 times that of the model plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (~135 Mb) while approximately same size as that of <i>Oryza sativa</i> (~420 Mb) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="The Tomato Genome Consortium: The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 2012, 485: doi:10.1038/nature11119." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2116">47</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833. doi:10.1038/ncomms4833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2119">60</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Initiative TAG. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature. 2000;408:796–815." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2122">43</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK, Li S, Liu B, et al. A Draft Sequence of the Rice Genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science. 2002;296(5565):79–92." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2126">61</a>].</p><p>Besides the saturated genome sequence, this investigation also provides an assembled chloroplast genome, showing highest similarity to that of <i>S. miltiorrhiza</i>, an important medicinal plant of traditional Chinese medicine. Both the plants are rich in phenylpropanoids and their derivatives, and many of these are implicated for different therapeutic activities. The presence of large number of homologs of copalyl diphosphate synthases (CPS) in <i>O. sanctum</i> genome indicates the possibility of finding newer diterpenes having potential bioactivity not implicated so far. Genomic information generated in this investigation not only is an important resource for evolutionary studies it will also catalyze modern genetic research by augmenting the data available for plant comparative genomics. This will also accelerate identification of important secondary metabolite-synthesizing genes, not identified yet from this medicinal and aromatic plant. Specific pathway related genes identified or mined in this genome could be used for the production of secondary metabolites following synthetic biology approaches. Genetic markers can be developed based on these genome sequences for studies involving genetic map construction, positional cloning, strain identification and marker-assisted selection. These molecular tools and genomic resources will accelerate molecular breeding and ultimately Holy basil’s utility in medical community.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Plant material, DNA preparation</h3><p>Leaf tissues of <i>O. sanctum</i> L. (variety CIM Ayu) were collected from the experimental farm at the CSIR-Central Institute of Medicinal and Aromatic Plants. High molecular weight genomic DNA isolated (Plant DNA extraction kit, Qiagen) from the leaves of <i>O. sanctum</i> was analyzed for its concentration and integrity. This DNA was then used for a whole-genome shotgun and mate-pair library preparation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Library preparation methods</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Long and short shot gun library construction</h4><p>Long and short insert libraries for whole genome sequencing were constructed as per Illumina TruSeq DNA library (TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Guide, Part No. 15005180 Rev. A, Nov 2010). 2 microgram of genomic DNA was used to prepare the DNA library acoustic shearing (Covaris Inc., USA) to a fragment distribution ranging between 150 to 600 bp and purified (Agencourt Ampure XP SPRI beads, Beckman Coulter, Inc.). Fragment distribution was analyzed (high sensitivity bioanalyzer chip, Agilent Technologies), finally purified (Agencourt Ampure XP SPRI beads) and quantified (Qubit fluorometer, Invitrogen as well as a high sensitivity bioanalyzer Chip, Agilent Technologies). The library shows a peak at the range of 300-400bp for short insert and 500-600bp for long insert libraries, respectively. Finally the libraries prepared were found suitable for 100bp paired end sequencing on Illumina.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Long reads 454 GS FLX library library construction</h4><p>454 GS FLX library was constructed according to the Roche rapid library preparation method manual (GS FLX+ Series—XL+, May 2011). Briefly ~1ug genomic DNA was fragmented (using a nebulizer), purified (Minelute PCR purification kit, Qiagen) and end-repaired followed by adapter ligation. The prepared library was validated for quality (high sensitivity bioanalyzer chip, Agilent Technologies) which showed an expected peak range of 1.4–1.8 kb.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Mate-pair library construction</h4><p>Mate pair libraries were generated as per the SOLiD Mate Pair Library preparation protocol. 23ug genomic DNA was sheared (ultra-sonicator, Covaris, USA) and analyzed for the size distribution (high sensitivity bioanalyzer chip, Agilent Technologies) also verified on 2 % E-gel. Next step was end-repairing of the fragments ranging from 2.5 to 3.5kb (resolved on 0.6 % agarose) followed by MPR-MPL adaptor ligation. Further, nick-translation was performed on circularized adaptor ligated DNA digested with T7 endonuclease I followed by S1 Nuclease enzymes. These products were 3’ adenylation by P1-T and P2-T, and captured using streptavidin beads (Invtirogen). Adaptor ligated sample was amplified with 18 cycles of PCR and size selected in the range of 250bp to 350bp using E-Gel (Invitrogen).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Sequencing of shot-gun and mate-pair libraries and Genome assembly</h3><p>Long and short insert libraries, were sequence on Hiseq2000 (Illumina) using 100 base paired end chemistry. Long single end reads were generated using Roche 454 (Roche) and mate-pair libraries were run on SOLiD 5500XL (Life technologies). Illumina generated 224,617,107 paired end reads (45.37 Gb data), 454 sequencing resulted in 643,134 single end reads (320.3Mb data) while SOLiD generated 126,824,255 mate pair reads (12.68 Gb data)</p><p>Long and short reads paired-end read data (HiSeq2000) of 449,234,214 (449 million) reads with high quality ( &gt; = Q30) were assembled with Edena v3.1 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Hernandez D, François P, Farinelli L, Osterås M, Schrenzel J. De novo bacterial genome sequencing: millions of very short reads assembled on a desktop computer. Genome Res. 2008;18:802–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2196">62</a>]. Edena was used with default parameters, i.e. minimum overlap size being 50 and coverage cutoff, 4. Total genome coverage from the long and short insert paired-end reads was ~18.25X and ~82.55X (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM1">1</a>), respectively. 643,134 long single end 454 reads, processed for quality filtering with Phred score &gt; =Q20 having a genome coverage of ~0.71X were then used for contig extension using SSPACE-2.03 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W. SSPACE: Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics. 2011;27(4):578–9. doi:10.1093/bioinformatics/btq683." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2202">63</a>]. SSPACE was used with these parameters: (i) minimum number of overlapping bases with the seed: 45, (ii) minimum overlap required between contigs to merge adjacent contigs in a scaffold:50, (iii) minimum number of read pairs to compute scaffold: 5 and contig extension switched on (iv) minimum number of reads needed to call a base during an extension: 20 and, (v) maximum number of allowed gaps during mapping with Bowtie: 1. Scaffolds thus generated do consisted of uncalled bases (Ns). Gap filling of these inter-scaffold Ns with nucleotides was carried out using GapClosure tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Luo R, Liu B, Xie Y, Li Z, Huang W, Yuan J, et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience. 2012;1(1):18. doi:10.1186/2047-217X-1-18." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2205">64</a>]. 252 million reads were generated using SOLiD showed ~30X coverage on the genome. SOLiD reads, with mean quality of Q20, and reads that have any uncalled bases (Ns) were filtered using SOPRA v1.4.6 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Dayarian A, Michael TP, Sengupta AM. SOPRA: scaffolding algorithm for paired reads via statistical optimization. BMC Bioinformatics. 2010;11:345." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2208">65</a>] tool. Super-scaffolding was performed in order to merge the existing gap-closed scaffolds into super-scaffolds using relative orientation of SOLiD mate pair reads. Super-scaffolding using MIP-scaffolder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Salmela L, Mäkinen V, Välimäki N, Ylinen J, Ukkonen E. Fast scaffolding with small independent mixed integer programs. Bioinformatics. 2011;27(23):3259–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2212">66</a>] requires F3 and R3 reads to be mapped on preassembled scaffolds. This was achieved using SHRiMP2 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="David M, Dzamba M, Lister D, Ilie L, Brudno M. SHRiMP2: sensitive yet practical SHort Read Mapping. Bioinformatics. 2011;27(7):1011–2. doi:10.1093/bioinformatics/btr046." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2215">67</a>] tool, which aligns reads in colorspace format.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>
                  <i>Ab initio</i> Gene model prediction was performed on scaffold sequences greater than 500bp using gene prediction software AUGUSTUS v2.5.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stanke M, Steinkamp R, Waack S, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:W309–312. doi:10.1093/nar/gkh379." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2229">68</a>]. Parameters from <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> species which share the same sub-class (asterid) with <i>Ocimum sanctum</i> were applied as training sets. Gene annotation of predicted proteins was done by matching to NCBI Non Redundant database using BLASTP (ncbi-blastv2.2.26+) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Altschul SF, Madden TL, Schäffer AA, Zhang J, Zhang Z, Miller W, et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997;17:3389–402." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2242">69</a>]. Domain prediction for unannotated proteins was performed against Pfam (release 27) HMM signatures [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Punta M, Coggill PC, Eberhardt RY, Mistry J, Tate J, Boursnell C, et al. The Pfam protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012;40:D290–301." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2245">70</a>] using Pfam-A set with HMMSCAN option in HMMER 3.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Eddy SR. Biological sequence analysis using profile hidden Markov models (Version 3.0 March 2010). [&#xA;                    http://hmmer.org/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ]." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2248">71</a>] at default parameters. Further scaffold sequences greater than 500bp in length were matched for match to EST/mRNA sequences available for <i>Ocimum</i> in the NCBI databases. <i>Arabidopsis</i> sequences from TAIR database were also BLAST checked against the <i>Ocimum</i> scaffolds (greater than 500bp). <i>Nicotiana</i> and <i>Solanum</i> EST’s from NCBI database were retrieved and matched against the assembled scaffolds which had length greater than 500bp.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Comparative genomics and SSR prediction</h3><p>The comparison of scaffolds with the <i>Ocimum</i> sequences was carried out using blat- Standalone BLAT v. 34x12 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kent WJ. BLAT–the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 2002;12:656–64." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2279">72</a>] fast sequence search command line tool. A total of 23,368 EST and 52 mRNA were queried, with a match to the assembled scaffolds for 21,984 of the EST/mRNA sequences at greater than 90 % sequence identity. <i>Arabidopsis</i> sequences from TAIR as well as <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> EST’s from NCBI database were also blast checked against the <i>O. sanctum</i> scaffolds (greater than 500bp). Apart from the database annotation of the assembled scaffolds these were also queried for intron length, intron distribution and gene density determination using AUGUSTUS v2.5.5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Stanke M, Steinkamp R, Waack S, Morgenstern B. AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:W309–312. doi:10.1093/nar/gkh379." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2295">68</a>] with <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> as references.</p><p>Scaffold sequences of length less than 500bp as well as greater than 500bp were separately checked for simple sequence repeats (SSRs) using MISA tool (<a href="http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/">http://pgrc.ipk-gatersleben.de/misa/</a>). The sequences were checked for mono-repeats occurring at-least 10 times, di-repeats occurring at-least 6 times and tri/tetra/penta/hexa-repeats occuring atleast 5 times.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Annotation and <i>de-novo</i> assembly of chloroplast and mitochondrial genome data</h3><p>Processed short reads paired-end read data of 72,912,212 (72.91 million) reads were aligned using BOWTIE2-2.1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Langmead B, Salzberg S. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat Methods. 2012;9:357–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2326">73</a>] to “<i>Liquidambar formosana</i> (Accession no. KC588388.1), <i>Nandina domestica</i> (Accession no. DQ923117.1), <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Accession no. NC_000932), <i>Citrus sinensis</i> (Accession no. NC_008334), <i>Cucumis sativus</i> (Accession no. NC_007144), <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> (Accession no. NC_007944), <i>Helianthus annuus</i> (Accession no. NC_007977), <i>Nerium oleander</i> (Accession no. KJ953906.1), <i>Oenothera biennis</i> (Accession no. NC_010361), <i>Platanus occidentalis</i> (Accession no. NC_008335), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Accession no. NC_009143), <i>Spinacia oleracea</i> (Accession no. NC_002202), <i>Ximenia americana</i> (Accession no. HQ664594.1), <i>Ilex cornuta</i> (Accession no. HQ664579.1)<i>, Dillenia indica</i> (Accession no. HQ664593.1)<i>, Oxalis latifolia</i> (Accession no. HQ664602.1)<i>, Plumbago auriculata</i> (Accession no. HQ664581.1)<i>, Staphylea colchica</i> (Accession no. HQ664600.1)<i>, Lonicera japonica</i> (Accession no. HQ664582.1)<i>, Antirrhinum majus</i> (Accession no. HQ664592.1)<i>, Cornus florida</i> (Accession no. HQ664596.1)<i>, Ficus sp.</i> (Accession no. HQ664605.1) chloroplast genomes. Database annotation of EST/mRNA from NCBI datasets identified the mitochondria and chloroplast expressed proteins. These 122 scaffolds were annotated to potentially map to these sequences. The aligned reads were assembled using SPAdes-3.1.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Bankevich A, Nurk S, Antipov D, Gurevich AA, Dvorkin M, Kulikov AS, et al. SPAdes: A New Genome Assembly Algorithm and Its Applications to Single-Cell Sequencing. J Comput Biol. 2012;19(5):455–77. doi:10.1089/cmb.2012.0021." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2399">74</a>]. The assembled contigs were scaffolded using SSPACE-2.0 using all the four libraries Illumina data. Saffolds were gapclosed using Gapcloser-1.6. OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW) was used for generating graphical maps of plastid genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Lohse M, Drechsel O, Bock R. OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW) - a tool for the easy generation of high-quality custom graphical maps of plastid and mitochondrial genomes. Curr Genet. 2007;52:267–74." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2402">75</a>].</p><p>Similar procedure carried for mitochondria assembly except chloroplast genomes <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> mitochondria genome used as reference and scaffolding and gapclosing was done using MIP-Scaffolder [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Salmela L, Mäkinen V, Välimäki N, Ylinen J, Ukkonen E. Fast scaffolding with small independent mixed integer programs. Bioinformatics. 2011;27(23):3259–65." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2411">66</a>] using SOLiD data. Chloroplast Scaffolds greater than 10kb were filtered, ordered and joined with 2 N’s though using <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i> chloroplast genome. Annotation was carried from draft genome using DOGMA tool [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Wyman SK, Jansen RK, Boore JL. Automatic annotation of organellar genomes with DOGMA. Bioinformatics. 2004;20(17):3252–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2417">76</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Sequence divergence and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The 32 complete cp sequences representing the asterid lineage of angiosperms were downloaded from NCBI Organelle Genome Resources database (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#MOESM17">17</a>). The 63 protein-coding gene sequences were aligned using the Clustal algorithm [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Thompson JD, Higgins DG, Gibson TJ. CLUSTAL W: improving the sensitivity of progressive multiple sequence alignment through sequence weighting, position-specific gap penalties and weight matrix choice. Nucleic Acids Res. 1994;22:4673–80." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2431">77</a>]. For the phylogenetic analysis, a set of 63 protein-coding genes commonly present in the 31 analyzed genomes was used. Maximum parsimony (MP) and Maximum likelihood (ML) analysis was performed for the phylogenetic analysis and the tree was generated using MEGA 6.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Tamura K, Stecher G, Peterson D, Filipski A, Kumar S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis Version 6.0. Mol Biol Evol. 2013;30:2725–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2434">78</a>] software. In the analysis <i>Spinacia oleracea</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> were set as outgroups.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Genome annotation and pathway identification</h3><p>85,723 protein coding sequences were blasted against NR proteins GO (Gene Ontology) terms were assigned for each protein based on the GO terms annotated to its corresponding homologue in the NR database. Each annotated sequence may have more than one GO term, assigned either for different GO categories (Biological Process, Molecular Function and Cellular Component) or in the same category [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Ashburner M, Ball CA, Blake JA, Botstein D, Butler H, Cherry JM, et al. Gene ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat Genet. 2000;25(1):25–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2451">79</a>].</p><p>Nucleotide sequences of the predicted proteins from scaffolds were retrieved (BEDTools-Version-2.13.1) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Quinlan AR, Hall IM. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics. 2010;26(6):841–2. doi:10.1093/bioinformatics/btq033." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2457">80</a>] and mapped to KAAS [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Moriya Y, Itoh M, Okuda S, Yoshizawa AC, Kanehisa M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007;35:W182–5." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-015-1640-z#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d16613e2460">81</a>] server to match pathway datasets from curated model species. Homology driven match of KO ID’s to best hits was done with default parameters. Match to model dicot and moncot plants <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Oryza</i> were applied for pathway annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Data access</h3><p>Genomic data generated by all the three platforms of <i>O. sanctum</i> whole project are available at NCBI under accession numbers SRX760129, SRR1653607 (Illumina); SRX760132, SRR1653610 (454_GS_FLX) and SRX761338, SRR1654829 (SOLiD). The data was submitted by SRA submission portal with submissionID, SUB745374 and BioProject ID, PRJNA267195.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Availability of supporting data</h3><p>The data sets supporting the results of this article are included within the article (and its additional files).</p></div></div></section>

AKS, AK, VG conceived and designed the whole genome sequencing experiments. SP, CG, GR performed the whole genome sequencing experiments. SR, SP, CG, GR, FK, VG analyzed the whole genome sequencing data. AKS, FK, AKT, SR interpreted the analyzed data. SR, AKS and AKT wrote the manuscript. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec25" data-title="Additional files"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec25-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec25">Additional files</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec25-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>

                          </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM18"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-015-1640-z/MediaObjects/12864_2015_1640_MOESM18_ESM.xlsx" data-supp-info-image="">Additional file 18:</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>
                            <b>Pathways associated with predicted proteins from scaffolds of</b>
                            <b><i>O. sanctum</i></b>
                            <b>as predicted by KAAS server taking Arabidopsis and rice as reference organisms.</b>
